1184 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Riggle v. Riggle


no. 4707-A-1987


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF ERIE COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 193; 3 Pa. D. & C.4th 358



July 13, 1989, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [*1] Motion for special relief and joinder of additional party.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant wife filed a motion for special relief and joinder of an additional party in an action by the wife seeking to reinstate her medical care coverage in a divorce action that had been commenced by plaintiff husband.


OVERVIEW: The wife filed a motion for special relief and joinder of additional party. The motion for special relief requested a resolution of health care insurance issues pursuant to the Consolidated Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1985, (COBRA), 43 P.S. §§ 140-195 (1992). The wife was separated from her husband. The husband and wife both agreed that the wife would receive continued health care benefits from the husband's insurance. However, the insurer's position was that because the parties were separated it was not obligated to provide coverage for the wife and it was not responsible for the wife's outstanding medical bills. The wife's position was that coverage had to continue pursuant to COBRA and the company's plan because the parties were not yet divorced or legally separated as Pennsylvania did not recognize a state of legal separation. The court found that because Pennsylvania did not recognize the state of legal separation, the qualifying event had to be the parties' divorce; and thus, the insurer erred in denying coverage to the wife.


OUTCOME: The wife's request for special relief and joinder of the additional party was ordered.

 

1185 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Ludwig v. Osterland


no. 1261 S 1987


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF DAUPHIN COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 63; 5 Pa. D. & C.4th 340



June 28, 1989, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [*1] Motion for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a jury verdict that exonerated defendant, an automobile service and repair company, from liability and found plaintiff dump truck driver 70 percent negligent, the driver filed a motion for a new trial. The driver had filed a negligence action against the company, alleging that the company was negligent in failing to adequately repair the brakes on the truck. The driver had been hurt when the truck was hit by a train.


OVERVIEW: The issue at trial was whether the truck was on the tracks due to a failure of the braking system or due to the driver's negligent operation of the vehicle. Several witnesses, including the driver's employer (an original party to the action who was later dismissed), one of the company's employees, and a police officer, testified that the driver had not mentioned brake failure as the cause of the accident, either at a hospital after the accident or four days thereafter. The driver filed a motion to preclude the admission of the testimony under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7101, which was denied. The driver contended that the court erred in admitting the statements that he had made to the witnesses while he was confined to the hospital. Denying the motion for a new trial, the court held that § 7101(a)(1) limited the language of § 7101(a)(2) so that the exclusionary rule was applicable only to statements obtained by persons with adverse interests who took the statement for the purpose of negotiating a settlement or obtaining a release. Clearly, the rule did not apply to gratuitous statements made by the driver to his employer or a fellow employee during a courtesy visit at the hospital.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion.

 

1186 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. 229.765 Acres of Land


no. 158 of 1989


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF GREENE COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 239; 2 Pa. D. & C.4th 548



June 23, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor bank filed preliminary objections to an amended petition for condemnation and forfeiture brought by plaintiff Commonwealth of Pennsylvania against defendant landowners' property. The Commonwealth filed competing preliminary objections.


OVERVIEW: A civil forfeiture action under the Controlled Substances Forfeitures Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6801 et seq., was instituted by the Commonwealth to seize the landowners' property on which suspected marijuana plants were found. The bank intervened as a mortgage holder. The court found that the bank had standing to challenge the forfeiture by virtue of its lien on the property. The court granted the bank's demurrer on the ground that the former forfeiture act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 35, § 780-128 (Supp. 1988), did not apply because such act only subjected real property to forfeiture in limited instances that did not appear in the case. The court granted the bank's preliminary objection to strike paragraph six of the amended petition because the paragraph contained several material allegations and thus, did not comply with Pa. R. Civ. P. 1022. The court denied the bank's preliminary objections attacking other paragraphs because the objections were not requests for a more specific pleading. The court denied the bank's fourth preliminary objection regarding constitutional violations because the constitutionality of a statute could not be raised on preliminary objections.


OUTCOME: The court granted the bank's demurrer as to the former forfeiture act and granted the bank's preliminary objection requesting the striking of paragraph six of the amended petition. The court denied the bank's preliminary objections that attacked other paragraphs of the amended petition and the bank's fourth preliminary objection. Where it denied the bank's objections, the court granted the Commonwealth's motion to strike.

 

1187 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Gulnac v. S. Butler County Sch. Dist.


equity no. 89-012


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF BUTLER COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 247; 2 Pa. D. & C.4th 541



June 9, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, students and parents, petitioned for a writ of mandamus directing defendant school district through its school board to seek an injunction against the teachers' bargaining union in accordance with § 1003 of the Public Employee Relations Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 1101.201 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a breakdown in negotiations, the teachers called a strike. Upon the declaration of the school board that it would not seek an injunction, notwithstanding the resulting inability to obtain 180 days of instruction as mandated by the Public School Code, 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 15-1501, plaintiffs sought a declaration as to the constitutionality of § 1003 of the Public Employee Relations Act (PERA), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 1101.201 et seq., which allowed teacher strikes unless such strike created a clear and present danger or threat to the health, safety, or welfare of the public. Opining that to allow an interested party to determine when judicial intervention was needed in a labor dispute directly conflicted with the constitutional requirement of a thorough and efficient public education system under Pa. Const. art. III, § 14, the court held § 1003 of PERA unconstitutional as a direct contravention of Pa. Const. art. III, § 14. Moreover, the court concluded that by the provisions of PERA, the state legislature had failed to provide measures to ensure 180 days' attendance as mandated by the Code, which sufficed as a clear and present danger to the rights of the students.


OUTCOME: The court reaffirmed in toto and made a final order its temporary ruling, declaring unconstitutional the statute that granted teachers the limited right to strike.

 

1188 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Ketterer v. Commonwealth DOT


No. 4854


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


20 Phila. 95; 1989 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 69



May 26, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Ketterer v. Commonwealth, DOT, 133 Pa. Commw. 92, 574 A.2d 735, 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 281 (1990)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, an injured motorcycle driver, challenged an order granting summary judgment to defendant Pennsylvania Department of Transportation (PennDOT). At issue was whether the Pennsylvania Tort Claims Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 8522, was unconstitutional, vague, and overbroad or violated the due process rights of an injured party by requiring him to prove that prior written notice of a road defect was given to PennDOT.


OVERVIEW: The driver was injured when his motorcycle struck a pothole and went out of control on a state highway. Though he sued PennDOT for failure to properly maintain the roadway, PennDOT moved for summary judgment on the basis that the Act barred such claims against PennDOT unless the claimant showed that it had actual, written notice of the alleged defect prior to the accident. When the driver was unable to show such notice, summary judgment was granted. The driver challenged the ruling on the basis that the Act was unconstitutional, but the court rejected those challenges. Specifically, the court found that the driver's claims that the Act was "vague and overbroad" and "impermissibly sweeping" intended to assert a First Amendment claim but that no claim was presented because the Act did not infringe at all upon protected speech. Second, the court found that the driver's Due Process claim was meritless because the Act was rationally related to legitimate governmental objectives and the Commonwealth was entitled to strike a balance, as reflected therein, between the right of a driver to recover and the right of the Commonwealth to maintain fiscal security.


OUTCOME: The court approved of its prior order rejecting the motorist's constitutional challenges to the Act and granting summary judgment to PennDot.

 

1189 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Karlin


no. 1350 C.D. 1988


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF DAUPHIN COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 61; 5 Pa. D. & C.4th 321



May 18, 1989, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [*1] Post-trial motions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed post-trial motions after he was convicted of willful failure to file Pennsylvania state income tax returns for the years 1985 and 1986, a violation of 72 P.S. § 7353(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted his belief that neither he nor anyone else similarly situated was required by law to file a Pennsylvania personal income tax return. The sole defense presented was that his failure to file the returns was the result of a good-faith misinterpretation of the law. Following his conviction, defendant filed a motion in arrest of judgment or, in the alternative, a motion for a new trial predicated on the assertion that the Commonwealth presented insufficient evidence to establish the element of willfulness beyond a reasonable doubt. The court stated that the underlying premise of both the Commonwealth and the federal "willful" requirement was to separate those taxpayers who through negligence, inadvertence, or mistake failed to comply with statutory requirements from those who demonstrated an obdurate refusal to acknowledge what the tax code required. The record was replete with evidence which was inconsistent with a credible claim of good-faith misunderstanding of the requirements of the law.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's post-trial motions.

Multi-Family Council v. Philadelphia


no. 922 November term, 1983


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 154; 3 Pa. D. & C.4th 1

DISPOSITION:  [*1] Action for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief.

 

1191 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Multi-Family Council of Southeastern Pa. v. City of Phila.


No. 922


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


19 Phila. 381; 1989 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 37; 3 Pa. D. & C.4th 1



April 13, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Multi-Family Council v. Philadelphia, 3 Pa. D. & C.4th 1, 1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 154 (1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a multi-family council, brought an action for declaratory and injunctive relief seeking to have defendant city's condominium conversion privilege tax (condo tax) declared void and unenforceable. The council claimed that the Pennsylvania Uniform Condominium Act, 68 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3100 et seq., expressly preempted the city's authority to impose the condo tax.


OVERVIEW: The condo tax made the owner of a rental apartment building liable for the tax if he converted his building to condominiums. However, if the building was converted to cooperative ownership, no tax was due. The court held that the condo tax was unconstitutional. The Act provided that a real estate use law, ordinance, or regulation could not prohibit the condominium, which it would not impose upon a physically identical development under a different form of ownership. Clearly the condo tax imposed a burden on condominiums that it did not impose on property that was identical but for its form of ownership. In addition, the condo tax violated Pa. Const. art. 8, § 1, which provided that all taxes had to be uniform upon the same class of subjects. When there existed no legitimate distinction between the classes and the tax scheme imposed substantially unequal burdens upon persons otherwise similarly situated, the tax was unconstitutional. The city's purpose in enacting the condo tax was to prevent conversion of rental apartments to condominiums. That was a prohibited purpose. Therefore, the tax was invalid.


OUTCOME: The condo tax was declared unconstitutional and the judicial relief requested was appropriate.

 

1192 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Campbell


no. 270 of 1988


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF WARREN COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 279; 2 Pa. D. & C.4th 26



March 16, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants brought post-trial motions for a new trial and for arrest of a judgment of the court (Pennsylvania), which convicted both defendants of a violation of § 4304 of the Pennsylvania Crimes Code, "Endangering the Welfare of Children."


OVERVIEW: The defendants were the parents of a minor girl who became pregnant by a boy her parents were aware she was dating. Defendants argued there was insufficient evidence to sustain a verdict of guilt beyond a reasonable doubt defendants in fact endangered the welfare of their daughter. The daughter testified her parents were unaware that she was having sex. The doctor testified that he advised the mother that it was not advised to put a girl of 13 on birth control and that a pregnancy at that age would be harmful. The Commonwealth had argued that because the parents were aware of the fact their daughter was dating the father of her unborn child and because the daughter was only 13 years old, the parents were guilty of endangering their child from the harms of pregnancy at such a young age in allowing her to become pregnant. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that because the baby had not been born, there was no proof that actual harm had come to the daughter and therefore, the was not sufficient evidence to uphold the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motions for judgment non obstante veredicto.

 

1193 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Silcox v. Bower


no. 591-S-1987


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF DAUPHIN COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 287; 2 Pa. D. & C.4th 248



February 22, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, parents and their child, filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings in their wrongful birth action against defendant physicians.


OVERVIEW: The parents' child was born with catastrophic birth defects, including mental retardation and severe anomalies of the lower limbs and hands. The parents alleged that the physicians knew of these anomalies but failed to inform them prior to the child's birth. The parents claimed that had they known of these anomalies prior to her birth, they would have terminated the pregnancy. The child, through her parents, claimed that she would have preferred non-existence to a life of mental retardation. Three years after the child's birth, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 8305 (1988) was passed eliminating a cause of action for wrongful birth with a provision that gave it retroactive effect. At issue was whether the parents and the child had acquired a vested right in their action for wrongful birth. In denying the motion, the court held that there was right, under appropriate circumstances, to a cause of action for wrongful birth. Because retroactive application of § 8305 would have extinguished that right, it was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the parents' motion for judgment on the pleadings.

 

1194 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Main Line Paving Co. v. Board of Education


Nos. 0622, 5250


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


18 Phila. 463; 1989 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 4



February 17, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reprinted as amended at Main Line Paving Co. v. Bd. of Educ., 20 Phila. 1, 1989 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 57 (Pa. C.P., 1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractor applied to the court for a temporary restraining order against defendant board of education to prohibit the board from opening bids on a contract for demolition and asbestos removal at a certain school where the bid specification contained a minority set-aside provision.


OVERVIEW: The contractor was the low bidder at the initial bid opening for the project but was denied the bid because it did not comply with the minority set-aside provisions of the board. The board decided to rebid the project following an initial injunction issued in the matter but still included the provisions for set-aside. The court determined that there was absolutely no testimony that the contractor ever discriminated against anyone. The court held that the action was not frivolous. The contractor raised a significant challenge to the constitutionality of the board's set-aside program because all rigid quotas for minorities have been seriously questioned and considered "highly suspect" by the United States Supreme Court. The court held that the program was precisely the broad kind of set-aside the Supreme Court condemned. The court found that there was irreparable harm from a bid opening because it was impossible to measure the potential damage caused to the contractor if other bidders could see the amount of the contractor's bid a second time. Also, the order maintained the status quo.


OUTCOME: The court issued a temporary restraining order against the board and in favor of the contractor.

 

1195 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Main Line Paving Co. v. Bd. of Educ.


Nos. 0622, 5250


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


20 Phila. 1; 1989 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 57



February 17, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY: Main Line Paving Co. v. Board of Education, 18 Phila. 463, 1989 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 4 (Pa. C.P., 1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff paving company sought a temporary restraining order prohibiting defendant city board of education from opening bids on a contract for demolition and asbestos removal at a school because the bid specification contained a minority set-aside provision.


OVERVIEW: The paving company was the low bidder at the initial bid opening for the project, but was denied the bid because it did not comply with the minority set-aside provisions of the board. The board decided to rebid the project following an initial injunction issued by the court, but still included the provisions for set-aside. The court granted the temporary restraining order, holding that (1) the action is not frivolous, but raised a significant challenge to the constitutionality of the set-aside program; (2) there was irreparable harm from a bid opening because it was impossible to measure the potential damage caused to the paving company if other bidders could see the amount of its bid a second time; (3) the order maintained the status quo. The set-aside program was invalid because the general statistics of discrimination that were submitted were not sufficient, it was a highly suspect rigid quota system, and there was no showing of discrimination by the paving company.


OUTCOME: A temporary restraining order was issued prohibiting the board from opening bids on the contract for demolition and asbestos removal at a school.

 

1196 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Autohaus Lancaster


no. 1768 of 1988


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF LANCASTER COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 150; 4 Pa. D. & C.4th 69



February 13, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant automobile dealer sought review of a decision of appellee township's zoning hearing board (Pennsylvania), which directed the dealer to remove a vehicle from the roof of its property on the ground that it violated appellee township's sign ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The dealer had, for a number of years, placed a single car on its rooftop for advertising purposes. Several months after a new sign ordinance became effective, the dealer received a notice of violation of Manheim, Pa., Zoning Ordinance No. 1987-30, § 1706-A(4), and it appealed, arguing that § 1706-A(4) did not apply because the car was not a sign. The board, however, disagreed. The court, in affirming, found that the board committed no abuse of discretion or error of law. Among other things, the court found that a vehicle on the rooftop constituted a "sign," as defined in Manheim, Pa., Zoning Ordinance No. 1987-30, § 1701, because it was placed there as an advertisement. Furthermore, the court rejected the assertion that Manheim, Pa., Zoning Ordinance No. 1987-30, § 1706-A(4) was inapplicable, noting instead that a car on top of a building was "attached" to the building. In addition, the court found that the dealer was not entitled to claim a non-conforming use because it failed to prove that the practice was lawful prior to the enactment of the ordinance. Finally, the court determined that the ordinance was constitutional and was not an improper exercise of police power.


OUTCOME: The court denied the dealer's appeal and affirmed the board's decision.

 

1197 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Dickerson


no. 8988-86


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF DELAWARE COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1989 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 294; 2 Pa. D. & C.4th 297



February 10, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the court's judgment, which convicted him of rape, deviate sexual intercourse, and possessing an instrument of crime, and also sought review of the court's denial of his post-trial motions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant approached the victim after she left a party, put a knife to her throat, and forced her into an abandoned house, where he assaulted her. She eventually managed to escape and immediately told her brother what had happened, but the two were unable to locate defendant. The victim then spent the night at her boyfriend's apartment, and she reported the assault to police the next morning. Defendant argued that the court erred in refusing to allow him to question the victim regarding her sexual activities with her boyfriend after the attack, alleging that the information was relevant as to her credibility. The court, in affirming its denial of defendant's post-trial motions, disagreed. Noting that a victim's sexual history, whether before or after an attack, was only of minimal probative value, the court reasoned that Pennsylvania's Rape Shield Laws, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104(a), (b), were designed to prevent the prejudicial effect of just such evidence as defendant sought to introduce. In addition, the court found that the verdict was not against the weight of the evidence, noting that the jury had assessed the witnesses' credibility and had concluded that defendant was guilty.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's post-trial motions following his conviction.

 

1198 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Tina K.


no. 1985-978


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF MONTGOMERY COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1988 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 7; 6 Pa. D. & C.4th 18



November 30, 1988, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The minor daughter of respondent father was found ungovernable in a dependency hearing. At the placement review hearing, appellant, the Office of Children and Youth Services (OCY), accused the father of sexual abuse and requested a dependency adjudication, which was granted. Upon the filing of exceptions, however, the trial court reversed its determination of abuse. OCY appealed. The Superior Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed. OCY sought review.


OVERVIEW: At the dependency/abuse hearing, the child never testified, nor was any explanation offered by OCY for her absence from the courtroom. The father's counsel objected to the introduction of the child's hearsay statements and also raised the question of her unavailability. The hearsay statements were permitted into evidence pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5986. The trial court considered the statements against the father's denials and determined that OCY met is burden of proving sexual abuse against the father by clear and convincing evidence. Reversal of the adjudication order was based solely upon OCY's failure to either produce the child at the hearing or establish her unavailability. The court agreed that such failure on the part of OCY constituted a violation of the father's due process rights. The court held that fundamental fairness required that a parent accused of sexually abusing his child be given an opportunity to confront and cross-examine that child in dependency proceedings unless it was shown that the child was unavailable.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the superior court's determination and reversed the adjudication order.

 

1199 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Johnson


No. 0755


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


18 Phila. 418; 1988 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 37



September 28, 1988 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court were defendant's post-trial motions for a new trial. Defendant was found guilty by a jury of rape, indecent assault, and corrupting the morals of a minor, based on charges that he had sexually assaulted his 11-year-old niece behind some bushes in a public park. The assault was interrupted by a 13-year-old witness, who testified against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in excluding testimony from the niece's playmate that, sometime before the alleged sexual assault by defendant, the niece had confided in the playmate that the witness who discovered the sexual assault had also molested the niece. The court held that the evidence had been properly excluded under Pennsylvania's Rape Shield Law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104. The evidence would not have exculpated defendant, was clearly irrelevant to his alibi defense, and would have unnecessarily prejudiced the victim by permitting unproven allegations of prior sexual activity with third parties to be presented before a jury. The court concluded that defendant had been sufficiently permitted to challenge the niece's credibility thorough his questioning of her on the stand. Defendant also challenged the prosecutor's closing remarks, which inferred that the niece had never accused anyone other than defendant of touching her in a sexual manner. The court held that the prosecutor, knowing that evidence to the contrary had been excluded, should not have made such an inference. However, the court held that any error had been cured by the court's jury instructions.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motions for a new trial.

 

1200 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Estate of William A. Banks


No. 200


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA, ORPHAN'S COURT


18 Phila. 341; 1988 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 31



September 28, 1988 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Exceptant Department of Revenue challenged the judgment of the trial court (Pennsylvania), which sustained the executors' appeal from the department's decision disallowing certain inheritance tax deductions.


OVERVIEW: The executors had claimed deductions as administration expenses under § 611 of the Pennsylvania Inheritance and Estate Tax Act of 1961 for interest paid and payable on deferred federal estate tax, interest on unpaid state inheritance tax, and interest on loans used to pay federal and state taxes. The department disallowed the deductions, and its appeals board affirmed. The executors challenged the determination, and the lower court ruled in their favor. The department filed exceptions, and the court sustained the exceptions. The court held that the inheritance tax statute taken as a whole did not permit consideration of taxes as "reasonable administration expenses" otherwise allowable under § 611. Although the executors acted reasonably in deciding to utilize the deferred method of payment and the borrowing of funds to pay interest on the tax was legitimate, the deduction of interest on taxes from the value of the property transferred was not within the purview of the statute. As the executors were not entitled to deduct the interest payments, the department's decision was proper.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the department's exceptions and reinstated its decision disallowing certain inheritance tax deductions taken by the executors.
